<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2018 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-08-15"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Brassica rapa comprises several important cultivated vegetables and oil crops. Current reference genome assemblies of Brassica rapa are quite fragmented and not highly contiguous, thereby limiting extensive genetic and genomic analyses. Here, we report an improved assembly of the B. rapa genome (v3.0) using single-molecule sequencing, optical mapping, and chromosome conformation capture technologies (Hi-C). Relative to the previous reference genomes, our assembly features a contig N50 size of 1.45&#8201;Mb, representing a ~30-fold improvement. We also identified a new event that occurred in the B. rapa genome ~1.2 million years ago, when a long terminal repeat retrotransposon (LTR-RT) expanded. Further analysis refined the relationship of genome blocks and accurately located the centromeres in the B. rapa genome. The B. rapa genome v3.0 will serve as an important community resource for future genetic and genomic studies in B. rapa. This resource will facilitate breeding efforts in B. rapa, as well as comparative genomic analysis with other Brassica species. A detailed third-generation genome sequence has been published for Brassica rapa, which includes crops such as napa cabbage, turnips, and bok choy. Older technologies required researchers to stitch together many short sequences. Recently developed methods produce long, high-fidelity sequences. Xiaowu Wang at the Chinese Academy of Agricultural Science and the Shandong Provincial Key Laboratory of Protected Vegetable Molecular Breeding, Hongkun Zheng at Biomarker Technologies Corporation, and coworkers in China assembled the new version using 1498 sequences, compared to the 96,883 needed for the previous version. These methods provide a significant improvement in the level of detail, and permit mapping of highly repetitive regions, which were nearly impossible to map using older methods. The new sequence will aid breeding efforts, permit better comparisons with other Brassica genomes, and improve our understanding of genome evolution."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Brassica rapa comprises several important cultivated vegetables and oil crops. Current reference genome assemblies of Brassica rapa are quite fragmented and not highly contiguous, thereby limiting extensive genetic and genomic analyses. Here, we report an improved assembly of the B. rapa genome (v3.0) using single-molecule sequencing, optical mapping, and chromosome conformation capture technologies (Hi-C). Relative to the previous reference genomes, our assembly features a contig N50 size of 1.45&#8201;Mb, representing a ~30-fold improvement. We also identified a new event that occurred in the B. rapa genome ~1.2 million years ago, when a long terminal repeat retrotransposon (LTR-RT) expanded. Further analysis refined the relationship of genome blocks and accurately located the centromeres in the B. rapa genome. The B. rapa genome v3.0 will serve as an important community resource for future genetic and genomic studies in B. rapa. This resource will facilitate breeding efforts in B. rapa, as well as comparative genomic analysis with other Brassica species. A detailed third-generation genome sequence has been published for Brassica rapa, which includes crops such as napa cabbage, turnips, and bok choy. Older technologies required researchers to stitch together many short sequences. Recently developed methods produce long, high-fidelity sequences. Xiaowu Wang at the Chinese Academy of Agricultural Science and the Shandong Provincial Key Laboratory of Protected Vegetable Molecular Breeding, Hongkun Zheng at Biomarker Technologies Corporation, and coworkers in China assembled the new version using 1498 sequences, compared to the 96,883 needed for the previous version. These methods provide a significant improvement in the level of detail, and permit mapping of highly repetitive regions, which were nearly impossible to map using older methods. The new sequence will aid breeding efforts, permit better comparisons with other Brassica genomes, and improve our understanding of genome evolution."/>

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Horticulture Research"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="A detailed third-generation genome sequence has been published for Brassica rapa, which includes crops such as napa cabbage, turnips, and bok choy. Older technologies required researchers to stitch together many short sequences. Recently developed methods produce long, high-fidelity sequences. Xiaowu Wang at the Chinese Academy of Agricultural Science and the Shandong Provincial Key Laboratory of Protected Vegetable Molecular Breeding, Hongkun Zheng at Biomarker Technologies Corporation, and coworkers in China assembled the new version using 1498 sequences, compared to the 96,883 needed for the previous version. These methods provide a significant improvement in the level of detail, and permit mapping of highly repetitive regions, which were nearly impossible to map using older methods. The new sequence will aid breeding efforts, permit better comparisons with other Brassica genomes, and improve our understanding of genome evolution."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/m685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig1_HTML.png"/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Brassica rapa</i> comprises several important cultivated vegetables and oil crops. Current reference genome assemblies of <i>Brassica rapa</i> are quite fragmented and not highly contiguous, thereby limiting extensive genetic and genomic analyses. Here, we report an improved assembly of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome (v3.0) using single-molecule sequencing, optical mapping, and chromosome conformation capture technologies (Hi-C). Relative to the previous reference genomes, our assembly features a contig N50 size of 1.45 Mb, representing a ~30-fold improvement. We also identified a new event that occurred in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome ~1.2 million years ago, when a long terminal repeat retrotransposon (LTR-RT) expanded. Further analysis refined the relationship of genome blocks and accurately located the centromeres in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome. The <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 will serve as an important community resource for future genetic and genomic studies in <i>B. rapa</i>. This resource will facilitate breeding efforts in <i>B. rapa</i>, as well as comparative genomic analysis with other <i>Brassica</i> species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Brassica</i> comprises various economically important species, many of which are extensively cultivated around the world as oil crops and vegetables. The six <i>Brassica</i> species comprise the “triangle of U”<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Nagaharu, U. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of B. napus and peculiar mode of fertilization. Jpn. J. Bot. 7, 389–452 (1935)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4397e725">1</a></sup>, which includes the three diploid species <i>B. rapa</i> (A genome), <i>B. nigra</i> (B genome), and <i>B. oleracea</i> (C genome), as well as the three amphidiploid species <i>B. juncea</i> (A and B genomes), <i>B. napus</i> (A and C genomes), and <i>B. carinata</i> (B and C genomes). The <i>Brassica</i> genomes not only underwent an additional whole-genome triplication event after divergence from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e754">2</a></sup> but also shared very recent genome duplications. These features make the <i>Brassica</i> genus an interesting system for the study of genome evolution in polyploids.</p><p>The <i>B. rapa</i> genome was the first to be sequenced among the <i>Brassica</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e770">2</a></sup>. The first released genome draft, <i>B. rapa</i> genome v1.5, was created using a whole-genome shotgun strategy with Illumina short reads and facilitated genome assemblies of other <i>Brassica</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950 (2014)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4397e781">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4397e781_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4397e784">5</a></sup>. A more recent release, <i>B. rapa</i> genome v2.0 (ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e791">6</a></sup>), resulted from iterative updates with additional short read data. It was further updated to the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v2.5 after improving the scaffold order (<a href="http://brassicadb.org/brad/datasets/pub/Genomes/Brassica_rapa/V2.0/V2.5/">http://brassicadb.org/brad/datasets/pub/Genomes/Brassica_rapa/V2.0/V2.5/</a>). Since 2011, both genetic and comparative genomic studies in <i>Brassica</i> species have benefited from the <i>B. rapa</i> draft sequences. Due to the relatively recent whole genome triplication, the <i>B. rapa</i> genome harbors highly repeated sequences and complicated centromeric regions, making it difficult to assemble the genome with high accuracy using short read technologies only. The inaccuracy in assembly and the low contiguity of the current draft assemblies have limited applications in both genomic and genetic studies of <i>B. rapa</i>.</p><p>Transposable elements (TEs) play an important role in genome expansion and evolution. Based on their mechanism of transposition, TEs are categorized into class I (retrotransposons) or class II (DNA transposons) TEs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Wang, H. The contributions of transposable elements to the structure, function, and evolution of plant genomes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 505–530 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4397e818">7</a></sup>.Retrotransposons, especially those belonging to the long terminal repeat retrotransposon (LTR-RT) class, are the most abundant and diverse TEs in plant genomes. LTR-RTs can be primarily classified into two super families, <i>Ty1/Copia</i> and <i>Ty3/Gypsy</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wicker., T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4397e827">8</a></sup>. Although LTR-RTs are conserved in structure, significant variations have been observed, even among closely related <i>Brassica</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4397e834">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e837">6</a></sup>. In <i>B. rapa</i>, two waves of LTR-RT expansion were identified since the divergence of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e850">6</a></sup>. However, only one wave of LTR-RT expansion was identified in <i>B. oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4397e856">4</a></sup>. Unfortunately, using the current assemblies of <i>B. rapa</i>, previous studies were unable to identify other important features of TEs. Owing to the highly repetitive nature of TEs, improving the quality of the genome assembly will allow the detection of more TEs in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome.</p><p>Comparative analysis of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> using ancestral genomic blocks of the Ancestral Crucifer Karyotype (ACK)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC’s of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4397e876">9</a></sup> identified 71 of the 72 expected genome blocks (3 × 24) in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e883">10</a></sup>. According to their rate of gene loss (fractionation), genome blocks were classified as belonging to the LF (the least fractionated), MF1 (the medium fractionated), and MF2 (the most fractionated) subgenomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e888">2</a></sup>. Comparison to the ACK and alignment of centromere-specific repeats highlights the centromeric locations in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e895">10</a></sup>. Although the previous genome assemblies provide extensive information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e899">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e902">6</a></sup>, there is room for improvements in the accuracy of defining the relationships of the genome blocks and the locations of centromeres in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome.</p><p>Over the last few years, considerable progress has been made to improve the assembly of plant genomes through the use of single-molecule sequencing, optical mapping, and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Recently, several high-quality plant genomes were assembled using one or a combination of these technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dong, J. et al. Analysis of tandem gene copies in maize chromosomal regions reconstructed from long sequence reads. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 7949 (2016)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4397e913">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099 (2017)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4397e913_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427 (2017)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4397e913_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4397e916">14</a></sup>. The use of single-molecule sequencing reads can overcome the limitations of short-read sequencing by producing long reads of tens of kilobases (kb), which span the repetitive regions in <i>Brassica</i>. Thus, the de novo assembly of a new reference genome for <i>B. rapa</i> using such novel third-generation technologies is imperative.</p><p>Here, we present a vastly improved assembly of <i>B. rapa</i> using a combination of single-molecule sequencing (PacBio), optical mapping (BioNano), and chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) technologies. Our new assembly, <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0, achieves a high level of continuity and is of superior quality. We not only improved the relationship of genome blocks and provided accurate locations of the centromeres but also identified an additional LTR-RT expansion event in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome. The updated assembly can be utilized as a valuable resource for future genetic and genomic studies, as well as a new reference genome for <i>B. rapa</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>To guide genome assembly, we estimated the size of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome by flow cytometry using rice as a reference. We initially estimated that <i>B. rapa</i> has a genome size of 455 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S1</a>). Further investigation involving calculations for the total length of the consensus map generated based on BioNano data indicated a genome size of 442.9 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S2</a>). Both estimations were smaller than the previously reported size of 529<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Johnston, J. S. et al. Evolution of genome size in Brassicaceae. Ann. Bot. 95, 229–235 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4397e965">15</a></sup> or 485 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e970">2</a></sup>.</p><p>We assembled the <i>B. rapa</i> genome using ~57-fold coverage of PacBio sequencing subreads (~25.88 Gb), ~456-fold coverage of BioNano data (~207.70 Gb), and ~164-fold coverage of Hi-C reads (~74.64 Gb).The resulting assembly consisted of 1476 contigs, with a contig N50 of 1.45 Mb and a total length of 351.06 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). Subsequently, we detected discrepancies within 22 contigs using the Hi-C reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM3">S4</a>). Instead of removing these contigs, we split these at the conflict regions; the data for Contig01464 are shown as an example (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of comparisons of assembly and annotation for the three <i>B. rapa</i> genome assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>After scaffolding and estimating gap sizes using BioNano maps and mate-pair reads (from BRAD, <a href="http://brassicadb.org">http://brassicadb.org</a>), we obtained 1301 scaffolds with a scaffold N50 of 4.44 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). To assign the resulting scaffolds to their chromosomal positions, we anchored these scaffolds using the Hi-C data and the improved genetic map (see Methods). We anchored 298.19 Mb of sequence on ten chromosomes that included 200 scaffolds clustered by Hi-C data and 8 scaffolds assigned by the genetic map. Our final assembly, termed <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0, totaled 353.14 Mb of sequence with 396 gaps (2.08 Mb) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). The <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 is longer than v1.5 but shorter than v2.5.</p><p>To assess the quality of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0, we used various data sources. First, we validated the completeness of our assembly by searching for core eukaryotic genes (CEGs) using CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1199">16</a></sup>. A total of 247 out of 248 CEGs were complete, and 1 CEG was partial, indicating that all of the CEGs could be detected in our assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S6</a>). Next, the genome quality was tested by matching the sequences of expressed sequence tags (ESTs) of <i>B. rapa</i> (downloaded from dbEST at NCBI), which showed that 99.34% of the ESTs could be found in the newly assembled <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Contiguity improvement</h3><p>The <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 has improved contiguity in terms of gaps and contig sizes. The <i>B. rapa</i> genome v1.5 was generated from Illumina sequences, whereas more Illumina reads and a relatively small amount of PacBio sequence data were used for assembly v2.5. These two assemblies have limitations due to their fragmentation and low contiguity (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). By combining single-molecule sequencing, optical mapping, and Hi-C technology, <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 represents a ~27-fold (contig N50: 1446 Kb vs. 53 Kb, v2.5) and ~31-fold (contig N50: 1446 Kb vs. 46 Kb, v1.5) improvement in contiguity over the two previous assemblies (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>).We also assessed the size and quantity of gaps in each respective assembly. There were only 396 gaps in v3.0, including gaps of known (122 from BioNano and 74 from mate-pair data) and unknown sizes (190 from Hi-C scaffold joining and 10 from genetic map joining). Compared to the previous assemblies, v3.0 has ~10-fold (5.89 Kb vs. 60.59 Kb, v2.5) and ~7-fold (5.89 Kb vs. 40.09 Kb, v1.5) improvement in the size of gaps per Mb over the two previous assemblies (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). In terms of the number of gaps per Mb, v3.0 is superior to v2.5 and v1.5, respectively, with ~23-fold (1.15 vs. 25.98, v2.5) and ~35-fold (1.15 vs. 40.09, v1.5) fewer gaps per Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>To assess the contiguity and accuracy of scaffold ordering of the three versions of the <i>B. rapa</i> reference genome, we first reconstructed the genetic maps based on the three assemblies using the same set of resequencing data of a doubled haploid (DH) population derived from a cross of two heading Chinese cabbage lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Liu, J. et al. A high density linkage map facilitates QTL mapping of flowering time in Brassica rapa. Hortic. Plant J. 2, 217–223 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1248">17</a></sup>. We then assessed the locations of binmarkers on the genetic maps by integrating them with the corresponding physical maps. Of the 892 binmarkers in our assembly, 877 binmarkers (98.3%) were mapped in the genetic map. Our assembly agreed with the genetic map for 801 binmarkers (91.3%), indicating the high quality of v3.0 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig1">1</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S7</a>). However, we noticed that 76 (8.7%) binmarkers on chromosomes A05, A08, and A09 mapped to ambiguous locations in the genetic map. These regions contained repeated sequences, especially at centromeric regions, as described in the following analysis. However, these conflicting regions were covered by PacBio reads and/or BioNano maps; the data for chromosome A08 in v3.0 are shown as an example (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Integration of the physical and genetic maps of B. rapa genome v3.0."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Integration of the physical and genetic maps of <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="654" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The markers of the genetic map based on <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 are shown on the <i>x</i>-axis; the markers of the physical map of <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 are shown on the <i>y</i>-axis</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There were 1092 binmarkers on the genetic map of v2.5 and 866 binmarkers on the genetic map of v1.5. However, we could only map 88.7% of binmarkers (969 of 1092) and 92.3% of binmarkers (799 of 866) onto the genetic map of v2.5 and v1.5,respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S7</a>). We found that 15.1% of the binmarkers (166 of 969) in v2.5 were discrepant, including 146 binmarkers with disordered genetic and physical distances within the same chromosome (intrachromosome) and 20 binmarkers with inconsistent genetic and physical distances on different chromosomes (interchromosome) (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S3</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S7</a>). For v1.5, 10.0% of binmarkers (80 of 799) were discrepant, including 71 binmarkers of intrachromosome and 9 binmarkers of interchromosome (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S4</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S7</a>). However, v3.0 contained the least conflicting intrachromosomal binmarkers (8.7%, 76 of 877 binmarkers) and no discrepant interchromosomal binmarkers (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S7</a>), indicating that <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 has a higher contiguity than the two previous assemblies. Taken together, these independent validations suggest that <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 has the highest contiguity and the best ordering of scaffolds among the three <i>B. rapa</i> assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparison of genome annotation</h3><p>We predicted and annotated the gene models as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1326">6</a></sup>. We identified a total of 45,985 protein-coding gene models in v3.0, which represented 14.74% of the genome assembly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). In our assembly, 98.75% (45,411 of 45,985) of the genes were annotated on chromosomes, and only 1.25% (574 of 45,985) was located on scaffolds. The de novo annotated genes in v3.0 were named following the standard of gene model nomenclature for the <i>Brassica</i> reference genomes (<a href="http://www.brassica.info/info/genome_annotation.php">http://www.brassica.info/info/genome_annotation.php</a>). The number of gene models in the novel assembly is higher than that in v1.5 (41,020 genes) but lower than that in v2.5 (48,826 genes) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). To further evaluate the quality of the annotation, a comparison with the annotation of previous assemblies was performed using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Simão, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1344">18</a></sup>, which is based on a benchmark of 1440 conserved plant genes. Approximately 97.7% of these conserved plant genes were identified, and 1.7% were detected as fragments presented in v3.0 (Supplementary Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S11</a>).</p><p>A genome synteny analysis was performed among the three assemblies using SynOrths<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cheng, F., Wu, J., Fang, L. &amp; Wang, X. Syntenic gene analysis between Brassica rapa and other Brassicaceae species. Front. Plant Sci. 3, 198 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1354">19</a></sup> to identify syntenic gene pairs and tandem gene arrays. A total of 2077 tandem arrays (corresponding to 4963 tandem genes) were identified in v3.0. The same number of tandem arrays (2077 arrays corresponding to 5004 genes) was also detected in v1.5. An assessment of genome-wide synteny indicated that 1539 tandem arrays (corresponding to 3757 genes) in v3.0 were syntenic to 1494 tandem arrays (corresponding to 3670 genes) in v1.5. However, more tandem arrays (3535 arrays, 8002 genes) were identified in v2.5 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). We detected gaps in the regions of superfluous tandem genes in v2.5, whereas no gaps were found in either 3.0 or v1.5 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig2">2a</a>). These gaps may be the result of assembly errors produced by gap closing using PacBio reads in v2.5, which in turn led to the invalid annotation of tandem genes. For other tandem genes without gaps, we observed that single genes in v3.0 and v1.5 were annotated as two or more genes in v2.5 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Examples showing the invalid annotation of tandem genes in v2.5."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Examples showing the invalid annotation of tandem genes in v2.5.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="738" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> An example of a 25 bp gap (thin yellow bar indicated by the red arrow) between the genes <i>BraA01000818</i> and <i>BraA01000819</i>, indicating an invalid annotation in v2.5. <b>b</b> The genes <i>BraA02003894</i> and <i>BraA02003895</i> in v2.5 are annotated as a single gene in v3.0 (<i>BraA02g039730.3</i><i>C</i>) and v1.5 (<i>Bra020703</i>). Figures were plotted using GEvo (<a href="https://genomevolution.org/coge/GEvo.pl">https://genomevolution.org/coge/GEvo.pl</a>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>When taking each tandem array as a single gene locus, there were 43,099 genes remaining in v3.0, 44,359 genes in v2.5, and 38,093 genes in v1.5 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). We then performed a gene synteny analysis, which revealed that 39,858 genes (92.48%) in v3.0 served as counterparts to 40,442 (91.17%) and 35,464 genes (93.10%) in v2.5 and v1.5, respectively. After comparison of the annotated genes with those of the early versions, we identified 3241 version-specific genes in v3.0 compared to both v2.5 and v1.5. Of these, 2380 genes were supported by evidence from matching mRNA reads of <i>B. rapa</i> (from BRAD, <a href="http://brassicadb.org/">http://brassicadb.org/</a>), and 2295 genes were supported by protein sequences of other Brassicaceae species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S12</a>). In total, 89.10% (2888 of 3214) of the version-specific genes in v3.0 were supported by the mRNA data of <i>B. rapa</i> or the protein sequences of other Brassicaceae species, while only 10.90% (326 of 3214) of the genes were not supported.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">A new LTR-RT expansion event identified in the updated assembly</h3><p>We annotated TEs in v3.0 using the same methods as previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Cheng, F. et al. Epigenetic regulation of subgenome dominance following whole genome triplication in Brassica rapa. New Phytol. 211, 288 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1433">20</a></sup>. A total of 235,683 TEs were identified from 1244 families in v3.0, and 562 unique TE families were found compared to v2.5 and v1.5. In v3.0, TEs representing 37.51% (134 Mb) of the assembled genome, which was higher than in the previous assemblies (32.30%, 126 Mb, v2.5; 25.44%, 72 Mb, v1.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1437">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1440">6</a></sup>. In our novel assembly, the most abundant TEs are LTR-RT, which covers a total length of 57.64 Mb and represents 16.32% of the assembled genome. Non-LTR-RT repeats (LINEs and SINEs) account for 3.10% of our assembly (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S5</a>). We detected DNA transposons corresponding to 26.35 Mb, which make up 7.46% of the assembled genome assembly (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S5</a>). A complete list of identified TEs and repeats in v3.0 can be found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S13</a>. In addition, we identified a total of 1231 miRNAs, 1281 tRNAs, 2865 rRNAs, and 3737 snRNAs in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S19</a>).</p><p>In our current assembly, we annotated more LTR-RTs (57 Mb) compared to v2.5 (44 Mb) and v1.5 (18 Mb). We identified 51,062 nonintact LTR-RTs in v3.0. Further analysis revealed that 65.27% (33,672 of 51,602) of nonintact LTRs were located on the ten chromosomes, whereas 34.73% (17,922 of 51,602) of nonintact LTR-RTs were found on the unanchored scaffolds. Using the same method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1463">6</a></sup>, a total of 13,318 intact LTR-RTs were annotated in v3.0. However, there were only 4129 and 801 intact LTR-RTs in v2.5 and v1.5, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1467">6</a></sup>. Further analysis revealed that only 18.19% of intact LTR-RTs (2423 of 13,318) were located on the ten chromosomes, whereas most (81.81%, 10,895 of 13,318) intact LTR-RTs were found on the unanchored scaffolds in v3.0.The insertion time of intact LTR-RTs was calculated as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1471">4</a></sup>, which indicated that the <i>B. rapa</i> genome underwent three waves of LTR-RT expansion since it diverged from <i>B. oleracea</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig3">3</a>). These intact LTR-RTs had an average insertion age of 1.88 million years ago (MYA), with a median insertion age of 1.59 MYA. Furthermore, we found more intact LTR-RTs with different lengths in v3.0 compared to in v2.5 and v1.5 (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S6</a>).</p><p>With these intact LTR-RTs, a new LTR-RT expansion event was identified in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome. We designated 3155 intact LTR-RT insertion events from 0 MYA to 0.4 MYA as a “young expansion” with an average length of 8135 bp and an average insertion date of 0.2 MYA; 2283 intact LTR-RT insertion events from 1.0 MYA to 1.4 MYA as a “medium expansion” with an average length of 11,902 bp and an average insertion date of 1.2 MYA; and 1444 intact LTR-RT insertion events from 3.0 MYA to 3.4 MYA as an “ancient expansion” with an average length of 9823 bp and insertion date (Fig. 3).The young and ancient expansions correspond closely to the previously identified expansion events[6]; the medium expansion was first identified in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome and has a similar insertion time as that of the intact LTR-RT expansion event in <i>B. oleracea</i>[4]. Furthermore, 1778 Ty1/Copia-like LTR-RTs and 4179 Ty3/Gypsy-like LTR-RTs were identified in v3.0, which is much more than those identified in the previous assemblies (353 Ty1/Copia and 632 Ty3/Gypsy in v2.5, 260 Ty1/Copia and 162 Ty3/Gypsy in v1.5) (Supplementary Table S20; Supplementary Figure S7, S8). In general, there were more Ty3/Gypsy-like LTR-RTs than Ty1/Copia-like LTR-RTs (Supplementary Table S20). Compared to v2.5 and v1.5, Ty3/Gypsy-like LTR-RTs in v3.0 were obviously increased since 5 MYA (Supplementary Figure S7), while Ty1/Copia-like LTR-RTs were increased since 2.2 MYA (Supplementary Figure S8). From the phylogenetic trees, we found that each group of LTR-RTs had more copies in v3.0 than in v2.5 and v1.5 (Supplementary Table S21, S22; Supplementary Figure S9, S10).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="420" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>The number of intact LTR-RTs birthed at different times (million years ago, MYA) in the three assemblies of the</b><b><i>B</i></b>. <b><i>rapa</i></b> <b>genome and in the genome of</b><b><i>B. oleracea</i></b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome blocks and centromeres in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome</h3><p>We investigated the relationships of genome blocks using the updated assembly v3.0. To define the genome blocks and centromeres in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0, we first constructed the three subgenomes (LF, MF1, and MF2) based on the syntenic relationship between v3.0 and <i>A. thaliana</i> (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S11</a>; Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM8">14</a>). We detected 71 out of the 72 (3 × 24) expected genomic blocks in v3.0, and most of them were arranged in line with those previously reported in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1545">10</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig4">4</a>; Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM9">15</a>). In v3.0, the two new fragmented genome blocks F (LF) and F (MF1) were identified on chromosomes A01 and A05 and were not observed in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1556">10</a></sup>. We could not detect two previously described, very small genome blocks in v3.0, block C (MF2) on chromosome A07 and block B (MF1) on chromosome A08 (ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1560">10</a></sup>). However, in our assembly, genome blocks N/M (MF1), O/P (LF), and A/C(LF) were arranged on chromosomes A01, A09, and A10, respectively, whereas they were ordered on opposite sides in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1564">10</a></sup>.The three small adjacent genome blocks(S (MF2), T(MF2), and B(MF1)) on chromosome A08 of v3.0 were ordered S/T/B, whereas these were arranged as T/B/S in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1568">10</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Distribution of genomic blocks along ten chromosomes of the B. rapa genome v3.0."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Distribution of genomic blocks along ten chromosomes of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="671" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Genome blocks on ten chromosomes were assigned to the subgenomes LF (red), MF1 (green), and MF2 (blue). Two or more segments of a single block were labeled using lowercase letters (a, b, etc.). The centromeres in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome are shown as black ovals, and the paleocentromeres are shown as gray triangles. Downward-pointing arrows are adjacent to GBs that are inverted relative to other blocks that originated from a single ACK chromosome</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also compared the orientation of genome blocks in v3.0 with that in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1592">10</a></sup>. The genome blocks W (MF2) on chromosome A02, as well as G (LF) and E (LF) on chromosome A07, were found to be inverted relative to the other blocks that originated from a single ACK chromosome. However, the orientation of genome block P (LF) on chromosome A09 and three blocks of V in v3.0 were in the forward direction, whereas these were inverted in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1596">10</a></sup>. These results were further supported by the genetic maps of v3.0 and v1.5, respectively.</p><p>We accurately determined the location of the centromeres of all chromosomes in v3.0. By screening previously determined centromeric repeat sequences, including centromeric satellite repeats CentBr, CRB, TR238, and PCRBr<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of rDNAs and tandem repeats in the heterochromatin of Brassica rapa. Mol. Cells 19, 436–444 (2005)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1603">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Koo, D. H. et al. Rapid divergence of repetitive DNAs in Brassica relatives. Genomics 97, 173 (2011)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1603_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of the centromere and peri-centromere retrotransposons in Brassica rapa and their distribution in related Brassica species. Plant J. 49, 173–183 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1606">23</a></sup>, we identified the signals for all 21 paleocentromericregions in v3.0, whereas three paleocentromeric regions were not detected in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1610">10</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM10">16</a>). Paleocentromere analysis indicated that the ten extant <i>B. rapa</i> centromeres were all inherited from the 21 paleocentromeres. In v3.0, the centromeres of chromosomes A01, A03, A04, A05, A06, A07, and A10 had the same associated genome blocks flanking the corresponding centromeres as reported in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1624">10</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig4">4</a>). However, the centromere on chromosome A02 was located between genome blocks P (MF2) and V (MF1), and the centromere on chromosome A09 was situated between genome blocks P (LF) and B (LF), whereas these were deemed paleocentromeres in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1631">10</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig4">4</a>). The centromere on chromosome A08 was located between genome blocks T (MF2) and B (MF1), rather than between genome blocks C (MF1) and T (MF2), as reported in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1638">10</a></sup>. Furthermore, there were 1188 genes detected within centromeric regions in v3.0, whereas only 740 genes were detected in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1642">10</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S17</a>).</p><p>To assess our assembly with regard to the centromeres in v3.0, we analyzed the sequence features of the centromeric regions. We found that a significantly higher number of TEs and centromere-specific repeats were mapped to the centromeric regions than to other parts of the chromosomes, and the gene density and recombination rate were markedly lower at the centromeric regions annotated in v3.0 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig5">5</a>). In addition, more centromere-specific repeats were detected at the centromeric regions in v3.0 in comparison to those reported in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1656">10</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S17</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Circos plot of the features of centromeric regions on the ten chromosomes in B. rapa genome v3.0."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Circos plot of the features of centromeric regions on the ten chromosomes in <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0071-9/MediaObjects/41438_2018_71_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="668" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>All the data are represented as heatmaps. The red color indicates low values, and the blue color indicates high values. <b>a</b> The ten chromosomes of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0. Centromeres are shown as black blocks. <b>b</b> TE density across the ten chromosomes of v3.0 (500 kb sliding window, 100 kb step). <b>c</b> Distribution of centromere-specific repeats along the ten chromosomes of v3.0 (2 Mb sliding window, 1 Mb step). <b>d</b> Gene density of the ten chromosomes of v3.0 (2 Mb sliding window, 1 Mb step). <b>e</b> The mean local recombination rate between markers along the ten chromosomes of v3.0 (5 Mb sliding window, 1 Mb step)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>We present the first long-read genome assembly of <i>B. rapa</i>. The advent of PacBio sequencing resulted in a dramatically improved contig N50 size compared to that of previous assemblies (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Tab1">1</a>). We note that PacBio reads tend to provide excellent results to fill gaps in assemblies stemming from short reads, as demonstrated by our current assembly and other studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1710">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bickhart, D. M. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and conformational capture enable de novo mammalian reference genomes. Nat. Genet. 49, 643–650 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1713">24</a></sup>. Optical mapping could not be performed using entire chromosomes due to the insufficiency of long-range spanning fragments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1717">5</a></sup>. Scaffolding with Hi-C facilitates the accurate assignment to chromosomal positions, as supported by our data on genetic markers.</p><p>Using the high-density genetic map derived from cross <i>B. rapa</i> lines with the same morphotype, we could accurately compare the quality of v3.0 with that of previous assemblies, indicating that our novel assembly achieved the highest contiguity and quality among the three assemblies. In previous assemblies, genetic maps derived from crosses between distantly related cultivars of <i>B. rapa</i> were used to assign scaffolds to chromosomes, which may have resulted in errors in the assembly. Although the length of v2.5 after the removal of “N”s was still longer than that of v3.0, it is possible that there were overlapping sequences in v2.5. In our assembly, most of the scaffolds were anchored by Hi-C data on chromosomes, and the remaining scaffolds were assigned using the genetic map. Although there were a few scaffolds that were syntenic to <i>A. thaliana</i>, we could not find any support from either Hi-C scaffolding or the genetic map. Further analysis suggested that there were more repeat sequences in these unanchored scaffolds, as we found fewer genes and more intact LTR-RTs in these scaffolds compared to those on chromosomes in v3.0. To alleviate this problem in the future, the length of input contigs using more PacBio sequencing reads could be increased or a restriction enzyme with a higher frequency of recognition sites for Hi-C scaffolding could be selected.</p><p>Our newly de novo annotated gene models were fewer than reported in v2.5. After removing the redundant tandem duplicated genes in each assembly, the number of gene models in v3.0 was closer in number to that in v2.5. A factor contributing to the observed decrease in gene number might be the overestimation of tandem duplicated genes in v2.5 due to the fragmentation of the genome assembly, which led to the annotation of sections of genes located on different contigs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig2">2</a>).The gene model nomenclature in v3.0 followed the standard for reference <i>Brassica</i> genomes (<a href="http://www.brassica.info/info/genome_annotation.php">http://www.brassica.info/info/genome_annotation.php</a>), making it possible to distinguish the genes in v3.0 from those of other lines of <i>B. rapa</i>.</p><p>As hotspots for TE insertions, centromeric regions are highly repetitive and have a relatively low gene density compared to the other parts of chromosomes. Thus, locating centromeric regions on a genome sequence is challenging, especially when using short reads as in the previous assemblies of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome. However, single-molecule sequencing substantially improved the assembly of repeats in v3.0, which allowed us to accurately define the locations of centromeres. Additionally, we annotated more genes within centromeric regions than in previous assemblies. These results not only enable us to investigate genes situated within these centromeric regions but also provide the basis for a functional analysis of centromeres in <i>Brassica</i>.</p><p>The ancestral genomic blocks along the ten chromosomes of <i>B. rapa</i> were previously reported by comparing the collinearity between the three subgenomes of <i>B. rapa</i> and the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1771">10</a></sup>. Although the previous assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1775">2</a></sup> provided much information on genome blocks, some blocks and block orientations were missing or reported as being inverted in ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1780">10</a></sup>, which was rectified in the new assembly (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#Fig4">4</a>). The refinement of the genome block arrangement in <i>B. rapa</i> will improve the resolution of interspecies comparisons of genome collinearity across <i>Brassicaceae</i>.</p><p>With the obvious improvement in contiguity in v3.0, a higher number of TEs were annotated than in previous assemblies of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome, particularly intact LTR-RTs. Further analysis identified a new LTR-RT expansion event in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome, which indicates differences in the features of LTR-RT amplification events between <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>. Moreover, more intact LTR-RTs might be detected and additional TE features may be identified in other plant genomes via long-read sequencing in the future.</p><p>Overall, our improved assembly, <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0, offers unprecedented insights into genome evolution and provides novel information relevant for comparative genome studies involving <i>B. rapa</i>. Finally, the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0 provides a solid foundation for future studies, not only in <i>B. rapa</i> but also in other <i>Brassica</i> species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">De novo genome assembly</h3><p>To estimate genome size, six biological replicates were analyzed by flow cytometry of <i>B. rapa</i> (accession Chiifu-401-42) using rice (<i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> cv. Nipponbare)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1847">25</a></sup> as an internal reference (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S1</a>). The genome size of an unknown ecotype of <i>B. rapa</i> was estimated at 529 Mb by flow cytometry without control analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Johnston, J. S. et al. Evolution of genome size in Brassicaceae. Ann. Bot. 95, 229–235 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1858">15</a></sup> and at 485 Mb for Chinese cabbage (accession Chiifu-401-42) using 17-mer analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1862">2</a></sup>. The estimated size of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome is very close to that of the BioNano consensus map, suggesting that previous studies may have overestimated its size.</p><p>The same <i>B. rapa</i> L. ssp. <i>pekinensis</i> in bred line (Chiifu-401-42) used for the earlier assemblies v1.5 and v2.5 was used for whole-genome sequencing in this study. High-quality genomic DNA from 500 mg of frozen leaf tissues was used to generate the PacBio libraries with an insert size of 20 Kb. The libraries were then sequenced in four Sequel cells (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA). Approximately 19.40 Gb of newly generated data and another 6.5 Gb of previous PacBio data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1878">6</a></sup> were incorporated into our genome assembly. Next, the PacBio subreads were de novo assembled using Canu (v1.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1882">26</a></sup> with default parameters. The Illumina reads obtained from BRAD (<a href="http://brassicadb.org">http://brassicadb.org</a>) were mapped to the PacBio contigs using BWA (v0.7.15)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1891">27</a></sup>.
