This alignment was then used to polish and correct the assembly by Pilon (v1.22)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome-assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1895">28</a></sup>.</p><p>The Hi-C libraries of <i>B. rapa</i> were constructed following the procedures described in a previous study with minor modifications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Grob, S., Schmid, M. W., &amp; Grossniklaus, U. Hi-C analysis in Arabidopsis identifies the KNOT, a structure with similarities to the flamenco locus of Drosophila. Mol. Cell 55, 678–693 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1905">29</a></sup>.The resulting libraries were submitted to an Illumina HiSeq 4000 sequencing device with 2 × 125 bp reads. Overall, we obtained ~584 million usable paired-end reads from two biological replicates. After alignment, ~27.87% of these read pairs could be uniquely mapped to the initial contigs.</p><p>The optical mapping (BioNano) data were generated using the BioNano Genomics Irys system (BioNano Genomics, CA, USA). The high-molecular-weight DNA was labeled by a specific nicking enzyme <i>Nt.BspQ1</i> (New England Biolabs, MA, USA) using the IrysPrep Reagent Kit (BioNano Genomics, CA, USA) as described by the manufacturer. Molecules were then filtered by a minimum length of 100 kb and a signal-to-noise ratio of 3.5. The filtered molecules were de novo assembled into a consensus physical genome map using the BNG IrysView analysis software package using manufacturer-recommended parameters for <i>B. rapa</i> (molecular length threshold: 100 kb; minimum label per molecule: 8; maximum backbone intensity: 0.6; false positive density/100 kb: 1.5; false negative rate: 0.15%; scaling SD: 0; site SD: 0.2 kb; relative SD: 0.03; initial assembly <i>p</i> value cutoff: 1e-8; extension and refinement <i>p</i> value cutoff: 1e-9; and merge <i>p</i> value cutoff: 1e-12; autonoise adjustment and 4 iterations of computation).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Conflict resolution</h3><p>To detect conflicts in the resulting PacBio contigs, we first aligned the Hi-C reads to these contigs and assembled by Lachesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1935">30</a></sup>. Contact maps for all contigs produced by Lachesis were drawn using the ggplot2 package (<a href="http://ggplot2.org/">http://ggplot2.org/</a>). We then checked the interaction signals for each sequence with the others. The detailed region in one contig was split when it had a strong signal with distant sequences (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM1">S1</a>). We could not find conflicts using BioNano maps because of the relatively short length of the contigs in our assembly. Once all the conflicts were resolved, the corrected PacBio contigs were used for scaffolding using the BioNano maps and mate-pair reads. Finally, the resulting scaffolds were used as input for scaffolding by Lachesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1946">30</a></sup> with parameters “CLUSTER_N = 10, CLUSTER_MIN_RE_SITES = 31, CLUSTER_MAX_LINK_DENSITY = 2, CLUSTER_NONINFORMATIVE_RATIO = 2, ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_TRUNK = 21,ORDER_MIN_RES_IN_SHREDS = 19”.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Construction of the genetic maps</h3><p>To construct the genetic map of our assembly, raw data of a DH population derived from a cross of two Chinese cabbage lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Liu, J. et al. A high density linkage map facilitates QTL mapping of flowering time in Brassica rapa. Hortic. Plant J. 2, 217–223 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1958">17</a></sup> were aligned to the assembled genome using BWA (v0.7.15)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1962">27</a></sup>. Only the uniquely mapped reads were used to call SNPs by SAMtools (v0.1.19)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1966">31</a></sup>. Recombination bins were constructed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Huang, X. et al. High-throughput genotyping by whole-genome resequencing. Genome Res. 19, 1068–1076 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4397e1970">32</a></sup> used as genetic markers and imported into JoinMap 4.0. Based on the locations of binmarkers, the genetic map was integrated with the physical map. To visualize the data, we plotted the genetic distance against the physical distance of the binmarkers for each chromosome. The same method was used to construct the genetic maps of v2.5 and v1.5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>We named the newly annotated gene models following the standards of gene model nomenclature for <i>Brassica</i> reference genomes (<a href="http://www.brassica.info/info/genome_annotation.php">http://www.brassica.info/info/genome_annotation.php</a>): Bra (for <i>Brassica rapa</i>) followed by the chromosome number and letter “g” (for gene). Genes from the top to the bottom of chromosomes were assigned numbers (in steps of 10) with five digits with leading zero integers. To distinguish the genes in v3.0 from the other lines of <i>B. rapa</i>, the number “3” (for the third version of <i>B. rapa</i> reference genome) and a single capital letter “C” (for variety Chiifu-401-42) were assigned after a “.” following the gene numbers; for example, BraA05g036760.3C.</p><p>After gene prediction, gene functions were assigned according to the best match of the alignments against various protein databases using BLAST v2.2.31 (E-value = 1e-5), including the KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2001">33</a></sup>, Swiss-Prot, and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2005">34</a></sup>. GO terms for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro entries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan—an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2009">35</a></sup>. Overall, we inferred 44,539 (96.86%) genes that were annotated based on the results from searching the protein databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM2">S18</a>).</p><p>Intact LTR-RTs were identified using LTR_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2019">36</a></sup> and classified the intact LTR-RTs by predicting the RT domains using the Pfam database (version 26.0) and HMMER software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Eddy, S. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol 10, 1–16 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2023">37</a></sup>. Muscle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and highthroughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2027">38</a></sup> was then employed to perform multiple RT sequence alignments, and RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 9, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2031">39</a></sup> was adopted to construct maximum likelihood (ML) trees based on the sequence alignments with 500 bootstrap replications. Finally, the interactive tree of life (iTOL)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive Tree Of Life (iTOL)v3: an online tool for the display and annotation of phylogenetic and other trees. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, W242–W245 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2035">40</a></sup> was used to plot the ML trees. The analysis of LTR insertion time was performed as previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2040">4</a></sup>.</p><p>We also performed noncoding RNA annotation for our assembly. tRNA annotation was conducted using tRNAscan-SE (v1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2047">41</a></sup> according to its structural characteristics. Homology-based rRNAs were localized by mapping known full-length plant rRNAs to the <i>B. rapa</i> genome v3.0. snRNAs were predicted by Infenal (v1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933–2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2054">42</a></sup> using the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Griffithsjones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 121–124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2058">43</a></sup>. miRNA annotation was performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Sun, C. et al. Impacts of whole-genome triplication on MIRNA evolution in Brassica rapa. Genome Biol. Evol. 7, 3085 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2062">44</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Genome blocks and centromere detection in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome</h3><p>We first constructed the three subgenomes (LF, MF1, and MF2) following the methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS ONE 7, e36442 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2079">45</a></sup>. Then, we defined the genomic blocks in v3.0 based on the syntenic relationship of the <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes. Centromeric repeat sequences, including those of CentBr, CRB, TR238, and PCRBr<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of rDNAs and tandem repeats in the heterochromatin of Brassica rapa. Mol. Cells 19, 436–444 (2005)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2089">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Koo, D. H. et al. Rapid divergence of repetitive DNAs in Brassica relatives. Genomics 97, 173 (2011)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2089_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of the centromere and peri-centromere retrotransposons in Brassica rapa and their distribution in related Brassica species. Plant J. 49, 173–183 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2092">23</a></sup>, were aligned to v3.0 using Nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4397e2096">46</a></sup>. The signals of the centromeric repeat sequences were used as evidence supporting the localization of the centromeres (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9#MOESM10">16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Data availability</h3><p>The data sets generated and analyzed during the current study are freely available through BRAD website (<a href="http://brassicadb.org/brad/datasets/pub/BrassicaceaeGenome/Brassica_rapa/V3.0/">http://brassicadb.org/brad/datasets/pub/BrassicaceaeGenome/Brassica_rapa/V3.0/</a>) or in the Genome Warehouse database under accession number GWHAAES00000000 (<a href="http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh">http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh</a>). All other data generated or analyzed during this study are included in this published article and its supplementary information files.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;dopt=Abstract&amp;list_uids=27595476" aria-label="View reference 5 on PubMed" rel="nofollow">PubMed</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 5 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=The%20genome%20sequence%20of%20allopolyploid%20Brassica%20juncea%20and%20analysis%20of%20differential%20homoeolog%20gene%20expression%20influencing%20selection&amp;journal=Nat.%20Genet.&amp;volume=48&amp;publication_year=2016&amp;author=Yang%2CJ">

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 46 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Versatile%20and%20open%20software%20for%20comparing%20large%20genomes&amp;journal=Genome%20Biol.&amp;volume=5&amp;publication_year=2004&amp;author=Kurtz%2CS">
                    Google Scholar</a> 
                </p></li></ol><p class="c-article-references__download u-hide-print"><a data-track="click" data-track-action="download citation references" data-track-label="link" href="/articles/s41438-018-0071-9-references.ris">Download references<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-download"></use></svg></a></p></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Ack1" data-title="Acknowledgements"><div class="c-article-section" id="Ack1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Ack1">Acknowledgements</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Ack1-content"><p>This work is supported by the National Natural Science Foundation of China (NSFC grants 31630068 and 31722048), the National Program on Key Research Project (2016YFD0100307), the Science and Technology Innovation Program of the Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences, the Key Laboratory of Biology and Genetic Improvement of Horticultural Crops, Ministry of Agriculture, P.R.                 <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Authors’ contributions</h3>
                <p>X.W. designed the project. L.Z., J.W., S.G. and J.L. prepared materials and performed the experiments. L.Z., H.Z., M.L., F.W., S.L., F.L. and X.L. performed the genome assembly. L.Z., X.C., F.C. and C.C. performed genome annotation. J.W., L.Z. Z.L. and B.L. constructed the genetic map. L.Z. and F.C. performed the analysis of genome blocks and centromeres. L.Z. and X.W. wrote the manuscript. U.G., S.G., J.W., F.C., L.C., W.Y. and M.-H.L. revised the manuscript. All authors discussed the results and commented on the manuscript.</p>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Genomes: a new map of field mustard</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>A detailed third-generation genome sequence has been published for <i>Brassica rapa</i>, which includes crops such as napa cabbage, turnips, and bok choy. Older technologies required researchers to stitch together many short sequences. Recently developed methods produce long, high-fidelity sequences. Xiaowu Wang at the Chinese Academy of Agricultural Science and the Shandong Provincial Key Laboratory of Protected Vegetable Molecular Breeding, Hongkun Zheng at Biomarker Technologies Corporation, and coworkers in China assembled the new version using 1498 sequences, compared to the 96,883 needed for the previous version. These methods provide a significant improvement in the level of detail, and permit mapping of highly repetitive regions, which were nearly impossible to map using older methods. The new sequence will aid breeding efforts, permit better comparisons with other <i>Brassica</i> genomes, and improve our understanding of genome evolution.</p></div></section></div></aside>
